<p>In most dialects, some verb participles used as adjectives have a separate form in both definite and plural uses,<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> and sometimes also in the masculine-feminine singular. In some Southwestern dialects, the definite adjective is also declined in gender and number with one form for feminine and plural, and one form for masculine and neuter.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Attributive_adjectives"><a href="/wiki/Attributive_adjective" class="mw-redirect" title="Attributive adjective">Attributive adjectives</a></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norwegian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Attributive adjectives"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Definite_inflection">Definite inflection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norwegian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Definite inflection"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>In Norwegian, a definite noun has a suffixed definite article (cf. above) compared to English which in general uses the separate word <i>the</i> to indicate the same. However, when a definite noun is preceded by an adjective, the adjective also gets a definite inflection, shown in the inflection table above. There is also another definite marker, <span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">den</i></span>, that has to agree in gender with the noun when the definite noun is accompanied by an adjective.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> It comes before the adjective and has the following forms
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<caption>Determinative <span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">den</i></span> (Bokmål)
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th>Masculine
</th>
<th>Feminine
</th>
<th>Neuter
</th>
<th>Plural
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">Den</i></span>
</td>
<td><span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">Den</i></span>
</td>
<td><span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">Det</i></span>
</td>
<td><span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">De</i></span>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Examples of definite affirmative inflection of adjectives (Bokmål):
</p>
<ul><li><span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">Den <b>stjålne</b> bilen</i></span> ('The <i><b>stolen</b></i> car')</li>
<li><span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">Den <b>pene</b> jenta</i></span> ('The <i><b>pretty</b></i> girl')</li>
<li><span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">Det <b>grønne</b> eplet</i></span> ('The <i><b>green</b></i> apple')</li>
<li><span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">De <b>stjålne</b> bilene</i></span> ('The <i><b>stolen</b></i> cars')</li></ul>
<p>If the adjective is dropped completely, the meaning of the preceding article before the noun changes, as shown in this example.
</p><p>Examples (Bokmål):
</p>
<ul><li><span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">Den bilen</i></span> ('That car')</li>
<li><span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">Den jenta</i></span> ('That girl')</li>
<li><span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">Det eplet</i></span> ('That apple')</li>
<li><span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">De bilene</i></span> ('Those cars')</li></ul>
<p>Examples of definite comparative and superlative inflection of adjectives (Bokmål):
</p>
<ul><li><span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">Det <b>grønnere</b> eplet</i></span> ('The <i><b>greener</b></i> apple')</li>
<li><span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">Det <b>grønneste</b> eplet</i></span> ('The <i><b>greenest</b></i> apple')</li></ul>
<p>Definiteness is also signaled by using possessive pronouns or any uses of a noun in its genitive form in either Nynorsk or Bokmål: <span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">mitt grønne hus</i></span> ('my green house'), <span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">min grønne bil</i></span> ('my green car'), <span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">mitt tilbaketrukne tannkjøtt</i></span> ('my receding gums'), <span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">presidentens gamle hus</i></span> ('the president's old house').<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Indefinite_inflection">Indefinite inflection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norwegian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Indefinite inflection"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Examples (Bokmål):
</p>
<ul><li><span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">En <b>grønn</b> bil</i></span> ('A <i><b>green</b></i> car')</li>
<li><span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">Ei <b>pen</b> jente</i></span> ('A <i><b>pretty</b></i> girl')</li>
<li><span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">Et <b>grønt</b> eple</i></span> ('A <i><b>green</b></i> apple')</li>
<li><span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">Flere <b>grønne</b> biler</i></span> ('Many <i><b>green</b></i> cars')</li></ul>
<p>Examples of comparative and superlative inflections in Bokmål: <span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">en grønnere bil</i></span> ('a greener car'), <span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">grønnest bil</i></span> ('greenest car').
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Predicative_adjectives"><a href="/wiki/Adjective#Predicative_adjective" title="Adjective">Predicative adjectives</a></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norwegian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Predicative adjectives"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>There is also predicative agreement of adjectives in all dialects of Norwegian and in the written languages, unlike related languages like German and Dutch.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> This feature of predicative agreement is shared among the Scandinavian languages. Predicative adjectives do not inflect for definiteness unlike the attributive adjectives.
</p><p>This means that nouns will have to agree with the adjective when there is a <a href="/wiki/Copula_(linguistics)" title="Copula (linguistics)">copula verb</a> involved, like in Bokmål: <span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">være</i></span> ('to be'), <span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">bli</i></span> ('become'), <span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">ser ut</i></span> ('looks like'), <span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">kjennes</i></span> ('feels like') etc. 
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<caption>Adjective agreement, examples
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th>
</th>
<th>Norwegian (bokmål)
</th>
<th>English
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>Masculine</b>
</td>
<td><span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">Bilen var grønn</i></span>
</td>
<td>The car was green
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>Feminine</b>

</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>Neuter</b>
</td>

</td></tr>
<tr>

</td>
<td><span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">Blåbærene blir store</i></span>
</td>
<td>The blueberries will be big
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Verbs">Verbs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norwegian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Verbs"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Nynorsk#Verb_conjugation" title="Nynorsk">Nynorsk §&#160;Verb conjugation</a></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485"><table class="box-Expand_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="[icon]" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" decoding="async" width="20" height="14" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="44" data-file-height="31" /></a></span></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Norwegian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">June 2019</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Norwegian <a href="/wiki/Verb" title="Verb">verbs</a> are not <a href="/wiki/Conjugation_(grammar)" class="mw-redirect" title="Conjugation (grammar)">conjugated</a> for <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_person" title="Grammatical person">person</a> or <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_number" title="Grammatical number">number</a>, unlike <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> and most <a href="/wiki/European_Languages" class="mw-redirect" title="European Languages">European languages</a>, though a few <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_dialects" title="Norwegian dialects">Norwegian dialects</a> do conjugate for number. Norwegian verbs are conjugated according to mainly three <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_mood" title="Grammatical mood">grammatical moods</a>: <a href="/wiki/Indicative_mood" class="mw-redirect" title="Indicative mood">indicative</a>, <a href="/wiki/Imperative_mood" title="Imperative mood">imperative</a> and <a href="/wiki/Subjunctive_mood" title="Subjunctive mood">subjunctive</a>, though the subjunctive mood has largely fallen out of use and is mainly found in a few common frozen expressions.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> The imperative is formed by removing the last vowel of the infinitive verb form, just like in the other Scandinavian languages.
</p><p>Indicative verbs are conjugated for <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_tense" title="Grammatical tense">tense</a>: <a href="/wiki/Present_tense" title="Present tense">present</a>, <a href="/wiki/Past_tense" title="Past tense">past</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Future_tense" title="Future tense">future</a>. The present and past tense also have a <a href="/wiki/Passive_voice" title="Passive voice">passive</a> form for the infinitive.
</p><p>There are four <a href="/wiki/Non-finite_verb" class="mw-redirect" title="Non-finite verb">non-finite verb</a> forms: <a href="/wiki/Infinitive" title="Infinitive">infinitive</a>, <a href="/wiki/Passive_voice" title="Passive voice">passive</a> infinitive, and the two <a href="/wiki/Participle" title="Participle">participles</a>: <a href="/wiki/Perfective" class="mw-redirect" title="Perfective">perfective</a>/past participle and <a href="/wiki/Imperfective" class="mw-redirect" title="Imperfective">imperfective</a>/present participle.
</p><p>The participles are <a href="/wiki/Verbal_adjective" class="mw-redirect" title="Verbal adjective">verbal adjectives</a>. The imperfective participle is not declined, whereas the perfect participle is declined for <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">gender</a> (though not in Bokmål) and <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_number" title="Grammatical number">number</a> like strong, affirmative adjectives. The <a href="/wiki/Definiteness" title="Definiteness">definite</a> form of the participle is identical to the plural form.
</p><p>As with other Germanic languages, Norwegian verbs can be divided into two conjugation classes; <a href="/wiki/Germanic_weak_verb" title="Germanic weak verb">weak</a> verbs and <a href="/wiki/Germanic_strong_verb" title="Germanic strong verb">strong</a> verbs.
</p>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ergative_verbs">Ergative verbs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norwegian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Ergative verbs"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Nynorsk#Ergative_verbs" title="Nynorsk">Nynorsk §&#160;Ergative verbs</a></div>
<p>There are <a href="/wiki/Ergative_verb" class="mw-redirect" title="Ergative verb">ergative verbs</a> in both Bokmål and Nynorsk,<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> where there are two different conjugation patterns depending on if the verb takes an object or not. In Bokmål, there are only two different conjugations for the <a href="/wiki/Preterite_Tense" class="mw-redirect" title="Preterite Tense">preterite tense</a> for the strong verbs, while Nynorsk has different conjugations for all tenses, like Swedish and a majority of Norwegian dialects. Some weak verbs are also ergative and are differentiated for all tenses in both Bokmål and Nynorsk, like <span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">ligge/legge</i></span>, both of which meaning 'to lie down', but <span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">ligge</i></span> does not take an object while <span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">legge</i></span> requires an object. <span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">Legge</i></span> corresponds to the English verb 'lay', while <span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">ligge</i></span> corresponds to the English verb 'lie'. There are, however, many verbs that do not have a direct translation to English verbs.
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<caption>Ergative verb <span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">knekke</i></span> ('crack')
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th>Norwegian Bokmål
</th>
<th>English
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">Nøtta <b>knakk</b></i></span>
</td>
<td>The nut cracked
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">Jeg <b>knekte</b> nøtta</i></span>
</td>
<td>I cracked the nut
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">Jeg <b>ligger</b></i></span>
</td>
<td>I'm lying down
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">Jeg <b>legger</b> det ned</i></span>
</td>
<td>I'll lay it down
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pronouns">Pronouns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norwegian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Pronouns"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Nynorsk#Pronouns" title="Nynorsk">Nynorsk §&#160;Pronouns</a></div>
<p>Norwegian personal <a href="/wiki/Pronoun" title="Pronoun">pronouns</a> are declined according to <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_case" title="Grammatical case">case</a>: <a href="/wiki/Nominative_case" title="Nominative case">nominative</a> and <a href="/wiki/Accusative_case" title="Accusative case">accusative</a>. Like English, pronouns in Bokmål and Nynorsk are the only class that has case declension. Some of the dialects that have preserved the <a href="/wiki/Dative_case" title="Dative case">dative</a> in nouns, also have a dative case instead of the accusative case in personal pronouns, while others have accusative in pronouns and dative in nouns, effectively giving these dialects three distinct cases.
</p><p>In the most comprehensive Norwegian grammar, <a href="/wiki/Norsk_referansegrammatikk" title="Norsk referansegrammatikk">Norsk referansegrammatikk</a>, the categorization of personal pronouns by <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_person" title="Grammatical person">person</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">gender</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_number" title="Grammatical number">number</a> is not regarded as inflection. Pronouns are a <a href="/wiki/Closed_class" class="mw-redirect" title="Closed class">closed class</a> in Norwegian.
</p><p>Since December 2017, the gender-neutral pronoun <span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">hen</i></span> is present in the Norwegian Academy's dictionary (<a href="/wiki/NAOB" class="mw-redirect" title="NAOB">NAOB</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> In June 2022, the Language Council of Norway (<a href="/wiki/Language_Council_of_Norway" title="Language Council of Norway">Språkrådet</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-:1_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> started including <span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">hen</i></span> in both Bokmål and Nynorsk Norwegian standards.
</p>

</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The words for 'mine', 'yours' etc. are dependent on the gender of the noun described. Like adjectives, they have to agree in gender with the noun.
</p><p>Bokmål has two sets of third-person pronouns. <span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">Han</i></span> and <span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">hun</i></span> refer to male and female individuals respectively; <span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">den</i></span> and <span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">det</i></span> refer to impersonal or inanimate nouns, of masculine/feminine or neutral gender respectively. In contrast, Nynorsk and most dialects use the same set of pronouns <span title="Nynorsk-language text"><i lang="nn">han</i></span> ('he'), <span title="Nynorsk-language text"><i lang="nn">ho</i></span> ('she') and <span title="Nynorsk-language text"><i lang="nn">det</i></span> ('it') for both personal and impersonal references, like in <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>, <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">Icelandic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a>. <span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">Det</i></span> also has <a href="/wiki/Expletive_pronoun" class="mw-redirect" title="Expletive pronoun">expletive</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cataphora" title="Cataphora">cataphoric</a> uses like in the English examples <i><b>it</b> rains</i> and <i><b>it</b> was known by everyone <b>(that) he had travelled the world</b></i>.
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<caption>Examples in Nynorsk and Bokmål of the use of the pronoun <i>it</i>

</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th>Nynorsk
</th>
<th>Bokmål
</th>
<th>English
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><span title="Nynorsk-language text"><i lang="nn">Kor er boka mi? <b>Ho</b> er her</i></span>
</td>
<td><span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">Hvor er boka mi? <b>Den</b> er her</i></span>
</td>
<td>Where is my book? <b>It</b> is here
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><span title="Nynorsk-language text"><i lang="nn">Kor er bilen min? <b>Han</b> er her</i></span>
</td>
<td><span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">Hvor er bilen min? <b>Den</b> er her</i></span>
</td>
<td>Where is my car? <b>It</b> is here
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><span title="Nynorsk-language text"><i lang="nn">Kor er brevet mitt? <b>Det</b> er her</i></span>
</td>
<td><span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">Hvor er brevet mitt? <b>Det</b> er her</i></span>
</td>
<td>Where is my letter? <b>It</b> is here
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ordering_of_possessive_pronouns">Ordering of possessive pronouns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norwegian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Ordering of possessive pronouns"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The ordering of possessive pronouns is somewhat freer than in Swedish or Danish. When there is no adjective, the most common word order is the one used in the examples in the table above, where the possessive comes after the noun, while the noun is in its definite form; <span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">boka mi</i></span> ('my book'). If one wishes to emphasize the owner of the noun, the possessive pronoun will usually be placed first. In Bokmål, however, due to its Danish origins, one could choose to always write the possessive first: <span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">min bil</i></span> ('my car'), but this may sound very formal. Some dialects that have been very influenced by Danish also do this; some speakers in <a href="/wiki/B%C3%A6rum" title="Bærum">Bærum</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Oslo_West" class="mw-redirect" title="Oslo West">west of Oslo</a> may always use this word order. When there is an adjective describing the noun, the possessive pronoun will always come first: <span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">min egen bil</i></span> ('my own car').
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tbody><tr>
<th>Norwegian (Bokmål/Nynorsk)
</th>
<th>English
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">Det er <b>mi</b> bok!</i></span>
</td>
<td>It is <i>my</i> book! (owner emphasized)
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">Kona mi er vakker</i></span>
</td>
<td>My wife is beautiful
</td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Determiners">Determiners</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norwegian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Determiners"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Nynorsk#Determiners" title="Nynorsk">Nynorsk §&#160;Determiners</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Closed_class" class="mw-redirect" title="Closed class">closed class</a> of Norwegian <a href="/wiki/Determiner_(class)" class="mw-redirect" title="Determiner (class)">determiners</a> are declined in <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">gender</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_number" title="Grammatical number">number</a> in agreement with their argument. Not all determiners are inflected.
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Particle_classes">Particle classes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norwegian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Particle classes"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Norwegian has five <a href="/wiki/Closed_class" class="mw-redirect" title="Closed class">closed classes</a> without inflection, i.e. <a href="/wiki/Lexical_category" class="mw-redirect" title="Lexical category">lexical categories</a> with grammatical function and a finite number of members that may not be distinguished by morphological criteria. These are <a href="/wiki/Interjection" title="Interjection">interjections</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_conjunction" class="mw-redirect" title="Grammatical conjunction">conjunctions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_conjunction" class="mw-redirect" title="Grammatical conjunction">subjunctions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Preposition" class="mw-redirect" title="Preposition">prepositions</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Adverbs" class="mw-redirect" title="Adverbs">adverbs</a>. The inclusion of adverbs here requires that traditional adverbs that are inflected in <a href="/wiki/Comparison_(grammar)" title="Comparison (grammar)">comparison</a> be classified as adjectives, as is sometimes done.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Adverbs">Adverbs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norwegian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Adverbs"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Adverb" title="Adverb">Adverbs</a> can be formed from <a href="/wiki/Adjective" title="Adjective">adjectives</a> in Norwegian. English usually creates adverbs from adjectives by the suffix <i>-ly</i>, like the adverb <i>beautifully</i> from the adjective <i>beautiful.</i> By comparison, <a href="/wiki/North_Germanic_languages" title="North Germanic languages">Scandinavian languages</a> usually form adverbs from adjectives by the <a href="/wiki/Neuter_(grammar)" class="mw-redirect" title="Neuter (grammar)">grammatical neuter</a> singular form of the adjective. This is in general true for both Bokmål and Nynorsk.
</p><p>Example (<a href="/wiki/Bokm%C3%A5l" title="Bokmål">Bokmål</a>):
</p>
<ul><li><span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">Han er <b>grusom</b></i></span> ('He is <b>terrible</b>')</li>
<li><span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">Det er <b>grusomt</b></i></span> ('It is <b>terrible</b>')</li>
<li><span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">Han er <b>grusomt</b> treig</i></span> ('He is <b>terribly</b> slow')</li></ul>
<p>In the third sentence, <span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">grusomt</i></span> is an adverb. In the first and second sentence <span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">grusomt</i></span> and <span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">grusom</i></span> are adjectives and must agree in grammatical gender with the noun.
</p><p>Another example is the adjective <span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">vakker</i></span> ('beautiful') which exists in both Nynorsk and Bokmål and has the neuter singular form <span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">vakkert</i></span>.
</p><p>Example (<a href="/wiki/Nynorsk" title="Nynorsk">Nynorsk</a>):
</p>
<ul><li><span title="Nynorsk-language text"><i lang="nn">Ho er <b>vakker</b></i></span> ('She is <b>beautiful</b>')</li>
<li><span title="Nynorsk-language text"><i lang="nn">Det er <b>vakkert</b></i></span> ('It is <b>beautiful</b>')</li>
<li><span title="Nynorsk-language text"><i lang="nn">Ho syng <b>vakkert</b></i></span> ('She sings <b>beautifully</b>')</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Compound_words">Compound words</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norwegian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Compound words"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In Norwegian <a href="/wiki/Compound_(linguistics)" title="Compound (linguistics)">compound words</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Head_(linguistics)" title="Head (linguistics)">head</a>, i.e. the part determining the compound's class, is the last part. If the compound word is constructed from many different nouns, the last noun in the compound noun will determine the gender of the compound noun. Only the first part has primary stress. For instance, the compound <span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">tenketank</i></span> ('think tank') has primary stress on the first syllable and is a masculine noun since the noun <span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">tank</i></span> is masculine.
</p><p>Compound words are written together in Norwegian, which can cause words to become very long, for example <span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">sannsynlighetsmaksimeringsestimator</i></span> ('<a href="/wiki/Maximum_likelihood" class="mw-redirect" title="Maximum likelihood">maximum likelihood</a> <a href="/wiki/Estimator" title="Estimator">estimator</a>') and <span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">menneskerettighetsorganisasjoner</i></span> ('human rights organizations'). Other examples are the title <span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">høyesterettsjustitiarius</i></span> ('Chief Justice of the Supreme Court', originally a combination of <i><a href="/wiki/Supreme_court" title="Supreme court">supreme court</a></i> and the actual title, <i><a href="/wiki/Justiciar" title="Justiciar">justiciar</a></i>) and the translation <span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">En midtsommernattsdrøm</i></span> for <i>A Midsummer Night's Dream</i>.
</p><p>If they are not written together, each part is naturally read with primary stress, and the meaning of the compound is lost. Examples of this in English are the difference between a green house and a greenhouse or a black board and a blackboard.
</p><p>This is sometimes forgotten, occasionally with humorous results. Instead of writing, for example, <i><span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">lammekoteletter</i></span></i> ('lamb chops'), people make the mistake of writing <span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">lamme koteletter</i></span> ('lame', or 'paralyzed', 'chops'). The original message can even be reversed, as when <span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">røykfritt</i></span> (lit. 'smoke-free', meaning no smoking) becomes <span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">røyk fritt</i></span> ('smoke freely').
</p><p>Other examples include:
</p>
<ul><li><span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">Terrasse dør</i></span> ('Terrace dies') instead of <span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">Terrassedør</i></span> ('Terrace door')</li>
<li><span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">Tunfisk biter</i></span> ('Tuna bites', verb) instead of <span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">Tunfiskbiter</i></span> ('Tuna bits', noun)</li>
<li><span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">Smult ringer</i></span> ('Lard calls', verb) instead of <span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">Smultringer</i></span> ('Doughnuts')</li>
<li><span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">Tyveri sikret</i></span> ('Theft guaranteed') instead of <span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">Tyverisikret</i></span> ('Theft-proof')</li>
<li><span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">Stekt kylling lever</i></span> ('Fried chicken lives', verb) instead of <span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">Stekt kyllinglever</i></span> ('Fried chicken liver', noun)</li>
<li><span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">Smør brød</i></span> ('Butter bread', verb) instead of <span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">Smørbrød</i></span> ('Sandwich')</li>
<li><span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">Klipp fisk</i></span> ('Cut fish', verb) instead of <span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">Klippfisk</i></span> ('Clipfish')</li>
<li><span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">På hytte taket</i></span> ('On cottage the roof') instead of <span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">På hyttetaket</i></span> ('On the cottage roof')</li>
<li><span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">Altfor Norge</i></span> ('Too Norway') instead of <span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">Alt for Norge</i></span> ('Everything for Norway', the <a href="/wiki/Royal_mottos_of_Norwegian_monarchs" title="Royal mottos of Norwegian monarchs">royal motto of Norway</a>)</li></ul>
<p>These misunderstandings occur because most nouns can be interpreted as verbs or other types of words.
Similar misunderstandings can be achieved in English too. The following are examples of phrases that both in Norwegian and English mean one thing as a compound word, and something different when regarded as separate words:
</p>
<ul><li><span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">stavekontroll</i></span> ('spellchecker') or <span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">stave kontroll</i></span> ('spell checker')</li>
<li><span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">kokebok</i></span> ('cookbook') or <span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">koke bok</i></span> ('cook book')</li>
<li><span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">ekte håndlagde vafler</i></span> ('real handmade waffles') or <span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">ekte hånd lagde vafler</i></span> ('real hand made waffles')</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Syntax">Syntax</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norwegian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Syntax"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Word_order">Word order</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norwegian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Word order"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Norwegian syntax is predominantly <a href="/wiki/Subject%E2%80%93verb%E2%80%93object" class="mw-redirect" title="Subject–verb–object">SVO</a>. The subject occupies the sentence-initial position, followed by the verb and then the object. Like many other Germanic languages, it follows the <a href="/wiki/V2_word_order" title="V2 word order">V2 rule</a>, which means that the finite verb is invariably the second element in a sentence. For example:
</p>
<ul><li><u><span title="Bokmål-language text"><span lang="nb" style="font-style: normal;">Jeg</span></span></u> <span title="Bokmål-language text"><span lang="nb" style="font-style: normal;"><b>spiser</b></span></span> <span title="Bokmål-language text"><span lang="nb" style="font-style: normal;">fisk</span></span> <span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">i dag</i></span> ('<u>I</u> <b>eat</b> fish <i>today</i>')</li>
<li><u><span title="Bokmål-language text"><span lang="nb" style="font-style: normal;">Jeg</span></span></u> <span title="Bokmål-language text"><span lang="nb" style="font-style: normal;"><b>vil</b></span></span> <span title="Bokmål-language text"><span lang="nb" style="font-style: normal;">drikke kaffe</span></span> <span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">i dag</i></span> ('<u>I</u> <b>want</b> to drink coffee <i>today</i>')</li></ul>
<p>Exceptions to the <a href="/wiki/V2_word_order" title="V2 word order">rule</a> are embedded clauses and question phrases.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Negation">Negation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norwegian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Negation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Negation in Norwegian is expressed by the word <span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">ikke</i></span>, which literally means 'not' and is placed after the finite verb. Exceptions are embedded clauses.
</p>
<ul><li><span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">Hunden kom <b>ikke</b> tilbake med ballen.</i></span> ('The dog did <b>not</b> return with the ball.')</li>
<li><span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">Det var hunden som <b>ikke</b> kom tilbake.</i></span> ('It was the dog that did <b>not</b> return.')</li></ul>
<p>Contractions with the negation, as is accepted in for example English (<i>cannot</i>, <i>hadn't</i>, <i>didn't</i>) are limited to dialects and colloquial speech. In this case contractions apply to the negation and the verb. Otherwise <span title="Norwegian-language text"><i lang="no">ikke</i></span> is applied in similar ways as the English <i>not</i> and general <a href="/wiki/Affirmation_and_negation" title="Affirmation and negation">negation</a>.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Adverbs_2">Adverbs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norwegian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Adverbs"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Adverbs follow the verb they modify. Depending on the type of adverb, the order in which they appear in the phrase is pre-determined. Manner adverbs for example, precede temporal adverbs. Switching the order of these adverbs would not render the phrase ungrammatical, but would make it sound awkward. Compare this to the English phrase "John probably already ate dinner." Switching the adverbs' position (<i>already</i> and <i>probably</i>) to "John already probably ate dinner" is not incorrect, but sounds unnatural. For more information, see <a href="/wiki/Cartographic_syntax" title="Cartographic syntax">Cartographic syntax</a>.
</p>
<ul><li><span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">Hun sang <b>rørende</b> <b>vakkert</b>.</i></span> ('She sang touchingly beautiful.')</li>
<li><span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">Hun sang <b>utrolig</b> <b>høyt</b>.</i></span> ('She sang unbelievably loud.')</li></ul>
<p>The adverb may precede the verb when the focus of the sentence is shifted. If special attention should be directed on the temporal aspect of the sentence, the adverb can be fronted. Since the V2 rule requires the finite verb to syntactically occupy the second position in the clause, the verb consequently also moves in front of the subject.
</p>
<ul><li><span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">I dag</i></span> <span title="Bokmål-language text"><span lang="nb" style="font-style: normal;"><b>vil</b></span></span> <u><span title="Bokmål-language text"><span lang="nb" style="font-style: normal;">jeg</span></span></u> <span title="Bokmål-language text"><span lang="nb" style="font-style: normal;">drikke kaffe</span></span>. ('<i>Today</i>, <u>I</u> <b>want</b> to drink coffee.')</li>
<li><span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">I dag</i></span> <span title="Bokmål-language text"><span lang="nb" style="font-style: normal;"><b>spiser</b></span></span> <u><span title="Bokmål-language text"><span lang="nb" style="font-style: normal;">jeg</span></span></u> <span title="Bokmål-language text"><span lang="nb" style="font-style: normal;">fisk</span></span>. ('<i>Today</i>, <u>I</u> <b>eat</b> fish.')</li></ul>
<p>Only one adverb may precede the verb, unless it belongs to a bigger constituent, in which case it does not modify the main verb in the phrase, but is part of the constituent.
</p>
<ul><li><span title="Bokmål-language text"><span lang="nb" style="font-style: normal;">Hun spiste suppen</span></span> <span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb"><b>raskt i går</b></i></span>. ('She ate the soup quickly yesterday.')</li>
<li><span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb"><b>I går</b></i></span> <span title="Bokmål-language text"><span lang="nb" style="font-style: normal;">spiste</span></span> <span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">hun</i></span> <span title="Bokmål-language text"><span lang="nb" style="font-style: normal;">suppen raskt</span></span>. ('Yesterday she ate the soup quickly.')</li>
<li><u><span title="Bokmål-language text"><span lang="nb" style="font-style: normal;">Laget som spilte <b>best</b></span></span></u>, <span title="Bokmål-language text"><span lang="nb" style="font-style: normal;">hadde forlatt plassen.</span></span> ('The team that played the best had left the pitch.')</li></ul>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Adjectives_2">Adjectives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norwegian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Adjectives"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Attributive adjectives always precede the noun that they modify.
</p>
<ul><li><span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">De <b>tre store tjukke tunge røde</b> bøkene stod i hylla.</i></span> ('The <b>three big fat heavy red</b> books stood on the shelf.')</li>
<li><span title="Bokmål-language text"><i lang="nb">Den <b>andre heldigvis lange tynne</b> nøkkelen passet.</i></span> ('The <b>other fortunately long thin</b> key fit'.)</li></ul>
